/Music Term/sequence// - Generally, this is a phrase repeated at a higher or lower interval. A real sequence is one in which the repeated phrase intervals are unaltered. A tonal sequcnce is one in which the repeated phrase is modified to prevent a key change.
/Music Term/recitative// - Generally, this is a style of singing used in opera and oratorio for dialogue and some narrative which is more closely related to dramatic speech in pitch and rhythm than to song. Two main types exist: (1) recitative accompagnato or stromentato which is expressive and accompanied by the orchestra and (2) recitative secco which has only an occasional broken chord from the harpsichord or 'cellos (sometimes with the bass line reinforced by the double bass) and this was the accepted style in 18th and l9th century operas.
/Music Term/consecutive intervals// - Harmonic intervals of the same kind (e.g. thirds succeeding one another in the same parts.
/Music Term/Missa solemnis// - High Mass.
/Music Term/soprano// - Highest female voice with range approximately extending from middle C to two octaves above that. Boy trebles can also achieve this range. The term is also applied to some instruments (e.g. soprano saxophone).
/Music Term/quarter note// - In 4 / 4 time, the equivalent of one beat.
/Music Term/tonal answer// - In a fugue, for example, after the first entry has been stated there is an answer (or second statement at a different pitch). If this answer is slightly modified to keep the music within a certain key, then this is a tonal answer and the fugue a tonal fugue. This is the opposite of real answer.
/Music Term/redundant entry// - In a fugue, this term describes an extra voice in the initial entries or exposition.
/Music Term/retardation// - In harmony this is a suspension which resolves upwards not downwards.
/Music Term/resolution// - In harmony this is the progression from a discord to a concord.
/Music Term/hocket// - In medieval church music, the insertion of rests into vocal parts for expressive purposes.
/Music Term/perfect time// - In medieval music, this was triple time.
/Music Term/melodrama// - In musical contexts, this term refers to the dramatic use of the spoken word against a musical background. This style may be used throughout an entire work or just as part of a work.
/Music Term/tie// - In musical notation this is a line joining two adjacent notes of the same pitch together, indicating that the first note only should be played but should be prolonged until the second note's time value is up.
/Music Term/expression marks// - Indications on the score provided by the composer to aid accurate performance of a work and consisting of dynamics (e.g. degrees of loudness and softness), tempo and mood.
/Music Term/naturale// - Instruction to a singer or instrumentalist to perform in the normal way (e.g. singing tenor instead of falsetto or playing without mutes).
/Music Term/MG// - Instruction to play with the left hand in piano playing. Abbreviation of main gauche (Fr.).
/Music Term/md// - Instruction to play with the right hand in piano playing. Abbreviations of main droite (Fr.) and mano destra (It.).
/Music Term/Transposing instruments// - Instruments that produce different notes than those written in concert pitch.
/Music Term/double counterpoint// - Invertible counterpoint in two parts occurring frequently in fugues.
/Music Term/triple counterpoint// - Invertible counterpoint in which three parts can be interchanged, each making a suitable bass for the other.
/Music Term/Legerement// - lightly.
/Music Term/metamorphosis of themes// - Liszt's term for leitmotif, the recurring and development of themes symbolising a character, emotion, object, idea, etc.
/Music Term/Mezzo forte// - Midway between loud and soft and abbreviated mf.
/Music Term/lament// - Music signifying grief but especially describing bagpipe music played at Scottish clan funerals.
/Music Term/programme music// - Music which interprets or describes a story, painting, poem, landscape or emotional experience. Opposite of absolute music.
/Music Term/nationalist// - Music with national characteristics (e.g. use of folk music. The term is particularly applied to 19th century composers (e.g. Smetena and Grieg. Bartok and Kodaly were also famous as nationalist composers).
/Music Term/monothematic// - Music with only one theme.
/Music Term/Key// - Musical term to indicate the tonality of a piece based on the major or minor scales and their relationship between the notes of the scale and chords built around them. There are two traditional types of keys (major or minor) depending upon whether they are based on the notes of the major or minor scale.
/Music Term/score// - Notated music of all performers' parts combined in an ordered form in which each part appears vertically above another. A piano score is one in which all orchestral or even vocal parts are reduced to a piano part. A miniaturce or pocket score reproduces all parts and details of the full score but is of a size more suitable for study.
/Music Term/G// - Note of the scale.
/Music Term/D// - Note of the scale; Abbreviation for Deutsch in the cataloguing of works by Schubert.
/Music Term/tonality// - Observance of a single key. Atonality, lack of key. Polytonality, the simultaneous use of several keys.
/Music Term/melodic minor// - One of the three types of minor scale.
/Music Term/minor// - Opposite of major. Applied to scales, keys, chords and intervals.
/Music Term/tremulant// - Organ device of producing a vibrato effect by alternately increasing and decreasing the wind pressure.
/Music Term/passacaglia// - Originally a slow and stately dance appearing in keyboard music of the 17th century. Later the passacaglia was a piece with a theme continually repeated, but not necessarily in the bass, like the chaconne.
/Music Term/foxtrot// - Originally an American dance in duple time of which there are two main typesÄfast and slow. It first became popular in 1912.
/Music Term/recapitulation// - Particularly used in sonata form, this term describes a section of a composition which repeats or approximates themes originally presented in a previous section, which have since been developed.
/Music Term/damping pedal// - Piano soft (left) pedal.
/Music Term/concord// - Pleasing to the ear and sounding harmonically resolved. However, interpretations of this term vary considerably. The opposite is discord.
/Music Term/patter song// - Popular in opera, this is a comic song in which words, sung as fast as possible, are often tongue-twisters. Often found in the works of Gilbert and Sullivan.
/Music Term/degree// - Position of note in scale (e.g. D is the second degree of C major scale).
/Music Term/double sharp// - Prefix attached to note indicating pitch to be raised by two semitones.
/Music Term/incidental music// - Properly, music to be performed during the action of a play. However, the term also includes overtures and interludes. 
/Music Term/enharmonic// - Refers to alternate naming or spelling of the same musical note
/Music Term/Da capo// - Repeat from the beginning.
/Music Term/Dal segno// - Repeat from the sign.
/Music Term/Da capo al segno// - Repeat to the sign.
/Music Term/Da capo al fine// - Repeat up to the word Fine (the end).
/Music Term/consonance// - Same as concord.
/Music Term/thorough bass// - Same as continuo.
/Music Term/dissonance// - Same as discord (see concord).
/Music Term/duo// - Same as duet but mainly an instrumental composition for two performers. A piano duo is for two pianists on separate pianos.
/Music Term/extemporisation// - Same as improvisation.
/Music Term/grace note// - Same as ornament, used to embellish a melody line and normally printed in smaller type.
/Music Term/embellishments// - Same as ornaments.
/Music Term/episodical form// - Same as rondo form.
/Music Term/dodecaphonic music// - Same as serial music.
/Music Term/tone poem// - Same as symphonic poem.
/Music Term/pantonality// - Schonberg preferred this term to atonality (e.g. music not written in any definite key).
/Music Term/imperfect cadence// - See cadence.
/Music Term/interrupted cadence// - See cadence.
/Music Term/discord// - See concord.
/Music Term/twelve note// - See serial music.
/Music Term/tuning// - set the temperament of an instrument.
/Music Term/modes// - Sets of eight-note scales inherited from ancient Greece via the Middle Ages in which they were most prevalent, although they still survive today in plainsong and folk music. At the end of the 17th century the modes had been reduced to two scales, major and minor, which we know today. Here are the modes which may be represented by scales of white notes on the piano with the names derived from the Greek system. The 'final' of a mode is the note of a cadence, or resting point, in a melody, and the 'dominant' is a reciting note.
/Music Term/landler// - Slow Austrian dance in waltz time popular in the late 18th and early l9th centuries.
/Music Term/mosso// - slower.
/Music Term/sinfonia// - Small orchestras currently perform under this name. Originally the title described an instrumental piece in the baroque era such as the prelude, overture to an opera, cantata or suite.
/Music Term/string orchestra// - Solely a string band as opposed to a wind or brass brand, normally consisting of first and second violins, violas, 'cellos and double basses.
/Music Term/elegy// - Song or instrumental composition for the dead.
/Music Term/Tonadilla// - Spanish stage entertainment involving a few singers.
/Music Term/corda// - String(s) - In piano playing, una corda (one string) indicates the use of the soft (left) pedal. The term tre corde (three strings) cancels this, indicating the release of the soft pedal.
/Music Term/impresslonlsm// - Term borrowed from painting, describing the works of Monet, Degas, etc. and transferred to music referring to the atmospheric music of Debussy and Ravel. A famous example is Prelude a l'apres-midi d'un faune by Debussy evoking the imagery of Mallarme's symbolist poem.
/Music Term/strophic// - Term describes a song which uses the same music for each verse.
/Music Term/polychoral// - Term describes the use of several choirs performing both separately and jointly in a composition.
/Music Term/preclassical// - Term describing music before Haydn and Mozart and also (more vaguely) describing music before Bach.
/Music Term/pointillism// - Term taken from painting (referring to pictures using separate dots of colour) and applied to the music of some 20th century composers (e.g. Webern). This described music of a spare and pointed style emplying use of pizzicato.
/Music Term/menuetto// - Term used by German composers who believed it to be Italian for minuet. The Italian for minuet is minuetto.
/Music Term/extemporization// - The art of spontaneous composition of music in performance. This may take the form of ornamentaion, variation of a song or theme, or completely new material. Improvisation is great]y used in jazz.
/Music Term/tenor clef// - The C clef on the fourth line.
/Music Term/ - The chord containing the sixth and fourth intervals from the bass note. Also known as the second inversion (e.g. the 6 / 4 chord G C E is the second inversion of C E G// - The chord containing the sixth and fourth intervals from the bass note. Also known as the second inversion (e.g. the 6 / 4 chord G C E is the second inversion of C E G
/Music Term/six-three chord// - The chord containing the sixth and third intervals from the bass note. Same as first inversion of a triad.
/Music Term/polymetrical// - The combining of different metres simultaneously (e.g. 2 / 4 against 3 / 4 or 6 / 8).
/Music Term/realisation// - The completion of 17th and 18th century harmony by adding a keyboard accompaniment indicated by figured bass.
/Music Term/part-writing// - The composing of polyphonic music and the writing of equally good melodic parts.
/Music Term/dynamics// - The degrees of softness or loudness in music indicated by signs or words on the score.
/Music Term/downbeat// - The downward movement of the conductor' s baton or hand indicating the first beat of the bar. The term can also mean the first beat of the bar.
/Music Term/half note// - The equivalent of two quarter notes or half of a whole note (two beats in 4 / 4 time).
/Music Term/tonic// - The first degree of the scale or a triad built on that degree.
/Music Term/exposition// - The initial statement of a musical theme or idea upon which a rnovement or piece is based. In a fugue, the exposition is the initial statement of the subject by each voice in turn. The exposition is completed when each voice has been heard for the first time. In sonata form, it is a repeated section in which the main themes are first stated before moving on to the development section.
/Music Term/octave// - The interval of eight steps (e.g. C to C on the white notes of the piano).
/Music Term/tritone// - The interval of three tones (e.g. F to B).
/Music Term/soft pedal// - The left foot pedal on a piano lessening the volume.
/Music Term/contralto// - The lower type of female voice having a range from F below middle C to G above the treble clef.
/Music Term/root// - The lowest or fundamental note of a chord. In the chord C E G, C is the root and the chord is said to be in root position. If the notes are arranged E G C, C is still the root, but the chord would be described as being in first inversion .
/Music Term/phrygian mode// - The mode which, on the white keys of the piano, is represented by E to E.
/Music Term/ionian mode// - The mode which, on the white keys of the piano, is represented from C to C (same as C major scale).
/Music Term/variation// - The modification or development of a theme, passage or figure with the theme always remaining recognizable.
/Music Term/progression// - The movement from one note or chord to another in music.
/Music Term/parallel motion// - The movement of two or more parts in the same direction while also keeping the same interval between them.
/Music Term/Ritmo di tre battute// - The music is to be performed in three bar groupings, implying that the music is so fast there is only one beat to the bar.
/Music Term/part// - The music of a particular voice or instrument in an ensemble.
/Music Term/sub-dominant// - The name for the fourth degree of the scale (e.g. F in the scale of C major) or the triad built on that degree.
/Music Term/tablature// - The notation in diagrams of guitar chords in pop music. Previously, it represented a method of notation involving symbols denoting the positions of the performer's fingers (e.g. for the lute.
/Music Term/final// - The note on which the melody ends in church modes. In authentic modes, the final is on the tonic. In plagal modes, it falls on the fourth degree of the scale.
/Music Term/note cluster// - The performance of a group of adjacent notes simultaneously on the piano, e. g. with the forearm or a piece of wood. Pioneered by Cowell in 1912 and used by Ives. Also known as tone cluster.
/Music Term/triple stop// - The playing of three notes simultaneously on a stringed instrument by the placing of the left hand fingers on the strings and shortening the vibrating length.
/Music Term/triple tonguing// - The rapid articulation of T-K-T on a wind instrument. This is difficult to do on reed instruments.
/Music Term/sustaining pedal// - The right pedal on the piano which raises the felt dampers allowing the strings to vibrate freely.
/Music Term/heptachord// - The scale of seven notes (e.g. the modern major or minor scale).
/Music Term/supertonic// - The second degree of the scale (e.g. D in the scale of C major) or the triad built on that degree.
/Music Term/development// - The section of a movement when initial statements of themes are expanded, developed, modified and broken up.
/Music Term/stave// - The set of five horizontal lines and spaces on which music is written. Leger lines are used for notes above and below the staff.
/Music Term/ode// - The setting of a poem entitled 'ode' to music.
/Music Term/leading note// - The seventh degree of the major scale. This is so called because it seems naturally to rise to the tonic a semitone above. In the minor scale this note is used only when ascending, not descending.
/Music Term/modulate// - The shift from one key to another in composition.
/Music Term/time signature// - The sign of figures at the beginning of a composition or section or movement indicating the number and kind of beats to the bar (e.g. 3 / 4 indicates 3 beats to the bar, with the number 4 signifying that the basic beat is a quarter note).
/Music Term/sub-mediant// - The sixth degree of the scale (e.g. A in the scale of C major) or a triad built on that degree.
/Music Term/glissando// - The sliding up or down a scale, often abbreviated by gliss. or a wavy or straight stroke between the highest and lowest note.
/Music Term/semitone// - The smallest interval commonly used in European music. On the piano this is represented by the interval between any note and the next note which may be higher or lower.
/Music Term/harmony// - The sounding together of notes in a musically significant manner. The main unit of harmony is the chord. The chords are built around the degrees of the scale. The primary chords in most popular compositions are based on the first, fourth and fifth degree of a scale. Many different theories have evolved over the past few centuries about the construction and interpolation of chords to create various senses of musical movement or intellectual or emotional responses. 
/Music Term/counterpoint// - The sounding together of two or more separate parts of rhythmic and melodic independence, in harmony. Invertible counterpoint occurs when any one melody strand can exchange its position for another (e.g. the bass becomes treble). Between two parts, this is called double counterpoint.
/Music Term/temperament// - The system of tuning intervals in order to fit them for practicable performance. The piano, organ and other fixed instruments are tuned to equal temperament which means that each semitone is made an equal interval so that (e.g. G sharp and A flat are the same).
/Music Term/progressive tonality// - The systematic plan of beginning in one key and ending in another in a movement.
/Music Term/polyrhythm// - The systematic use of quite different rhythms sounding simultaneously. Polyrhythmic devices are especially used in the 20th century.
/Music Term/partials// - The tones of the harmonic series, the lowest being thefirst partial and the others the upper partials or overtones.
/Music Term/diminution// - The treatment of a melody by shortening the time values of notes, usually by half (e.g. in fugues and canons).
/Music Term/inversion// - The turning upside-down of a chord or single melody (by applying intervals in opposite directions) or two melodies in counterpoint by the upper melody becoming the lower and vice-versa. This last method is called invertible counterpoint.
/Music Term/upbeat// - The upward movement of a conductor's hand or baton, especially indicating the beat before the main accent in a bar of music.
/Music Term/polytonality// - The use of two or more keys performed simultaneously and employed by Stravinsky, Holst and Milhaud. When only two keys are used, this is referred to as bitonality.
/Music Term/transposition// - The writing down or performing of music in a different key from the original. 
/Music Term/perfect intervals// - These are the intervals of the octave, fourth and fifth.
/Music Term/major minor// - These are the two main scales of the western tonal system. The major key is based on the major scale and the minor key is based on the minor scale. The minor scale breaks into three variations: harmonic, melodic and natural. These terms also refer to chords and intervals being built out of the major or minor scale. 
/Music Term/time// - This describes the basic rhythmical patterns in music (e.g. 6 / 8 time or 4 / 4 time). Also march time, waltz time, etc.
/Music Term/secondary dominant// - This describes the dominant of the dominant .
/Music Term/key signature// - This indicates the precise key of the piece. Sharps or flats are placed at the beginning of a composition after the clef. Any other alteration (e.g. a brief modulation to another key, is indicated by accidentals. If there is an extended passage in a new key, however, then a new key signature may appear. The key signature must be represented at the beginning of every new stave in a composition (although not always in popular music). 
/Music Term/metre// - This is indicated by a time signature dividing up the music into regularly occurring accents (e.g. 3 / 4 time means that the basic note values are quarter notes and that every third one is accented).
/Music Term/mass// - This is the main service of the Roman Catholic Church which has been set to music by composers including Bach, Mozart and Haydn.
/Music Term/passion music// - This is the Passion of Christ, as accounted by Matthew, Mark, Luke and John, set to music and properly performed during Holy Week.
/Music Term/note row// - This occurs in 20th century serial music, also called dodecaphonic music or twelve-note music. It is the order in which the composer chooses to arrange the twelve notes, which serves as the foundation of the composition. novelette (Eng.) or novellette (Ger.) - A short, instrumental, romantic piece. The term was first used by Schumann for a piano work in 1848.
/Music Term/lesson// - This term described a short, keyboard piece or a set of short pieces in the 17th and 18th centuries.
/Music Term/mirror// - This term is sometimes attached to a fugue or canon to describe two or more parts appearing simultaneously, with one the correct way up and the other upside down, as if a mirror had been placed between them.
/Music Term/relative// - This term refers to each key signature being shared by two keys (e.g. A minor is the 'relative' minor of C major since they both have no sharps or flats in the key signature. D major is the relative major of B minor since both keys have two sharps in the key signature.
/Music Term/rococo// - This term was taken from French culture in the early 18th century, but musically the title for rococo is the style galant used by composers throughout Europe until the late 18th century.
/Music Term/secco// - This word is associated with recitative.
/Music Term/triple time// - Time consisting of three beats to the bar (e.g. 3 / 4, 3 / 2, 3 / 8).
/Music Term/simple time// - Time in which each beat is divisible by two (e.g. 2 / 4, 4 / 4, 3 / 4 and in which each beat is a quarter note).
/Music Term/duple time// - Time in which the number of beats in the bar is divisible by two or four (e.g. 2 / 4, 4 / 4, 2 / 2).
/Music Term/quintuple time// - Time with five beats, usually quarter notes, to the bar (e.g. 5 / 4 time). Not common before the 20th century.
/Music Term/conduct// - To direct a performance of either singers, players or both with a baton or hands in order to give precise indications of dynamics, phrasing and speed.
/Music Term/scoop// - To slide up to a note in singing instead of hitting it accurately.
/Music Term/double bar// - Two bar lines placed closely together to signify the end of a composition or section.
/Music Term/similar motion// - Two melodies moving together in the same direction.
/Music Term/head voice// - Upper register of voice.
/Music Term/dolcissimo// - Very sweetly.
/Music Term/Mezza voce// - With a moderate tone.
/Music Term/notation// - Written music, by ordinary staff notation symbols or graphic representation or simply by letter-names (e.g. the tonic sol-fa.
/Music Term/ten// . - Abbreviation of (1) tenor and (2) tenuto.
/Music Term/six-four chord// sixteenth note - one fourth of a quarter note.
